Wood v. State


Case Number: D-2005-171


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2007 OK CR 17; 158 P.3d 467; 2007 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 17



April 30, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Wood v. Oklahoma, 552 U.S. 999, 128 S. Ct. 507, 169 L. Ed. 2d 355, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 11828 (2007)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Request denied by, Judgment entered by Wood v. Trammell, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 147184 (W.D. Okla., Oct. 30, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OKLAHOMA COUNTY. THE HONORABLE RAY C. ELLIOTT, DISTRICT JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: TREMANE WOOD, Appellant, was tried by jury and convicted of First Degree Murder, Robbery with Firearms, After Former Conviction of a Felony and Conspiracy, After Former Conviction of a Felony, in the District Court of Oklahoma County, Case No. CF-2002-46. The Honorable Ray C. Elliott, District Judge, presided and imposed the jury's verdict. He sentenced Wood to death for the murder and life imprisonment for robbery and conspiracy. Wood perfected this appeal. Judgment and Sentence is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which, according to a jury verdict, sentenced defendant to death for first degree murder in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.7(B) (2001) and life imprisonment for robbery with firearms in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 801 (2001) and conspiracy to commit a felony in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 421 (2001).


OVERVIEW: Defendant, his brother, and two women decided that the women would pretend to be prostitutes and that the brothers would arrive at the motel later to rob two men. One victim was stabbed to death during a struggle. The jury found that defendant knowingly created a great risk of death to more than one person, that the murder was especially heinous or cruel, and that defendant posed a continuing threat to society under Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.12 (2), (4) and (7) (2001). On appeal, the court found that Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 860.1 and Okla. Stat. tit. 21 § 701.10 (2001) did not prohibit the State from seeking to enhance defendant's noncapital offenses with proof of a prior conviction. The aggravating factors were not vague or broad as applied in violation of U.S. Const. amends. VII and XIV, and substantial evidence of the struggle and danger to the second victim substantially supported the factors. Defendant's ineffective assistance of counsel claim under U.S. Const. amend. VI failed because he could not show that the outcome of his case would have been different had the credible evidence developed at the evidentiary hearing been presented during his capital sentencing proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the sentence.

 

254 of 423 DOCUMENTS


City of Elk City v. Taylor


Case Number: SR-2006-1101


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2007 OK CR 15; 157 P.3d 1152; 2007 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 15



April 23, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review from a judgment of the District Court of Beckham County (Oklahoma), which after a trial de novo affirmed a municipal court's decision adverse to appellee on two counts of violating a city ordinance prohibiting inoperative or junk vehicles and reversed a finding of guilt as to four counts, dismissing those charges. The appeal was transferred to the court from the Oklahoma Supreme Court.


OVERVIEW: The city sought to reinstate the decision of the municipal court as to the reversed counts. Appellee argued that the appeal should be dismissed because the city did not properly comply with the requirements of Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 1053 for appeals by the state. The court dismissed the appeal. The city's appeal from a judgment of the district court on a trial de novo of a case appealed from a municipal court not of record for the violation of a city ordinance was a criminal matter under Okla. Stat. tit. 11, §§ 27-102, 27-103, and the city's right to appeal was not expressly authorized under § 1053. Additionally, neither Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 1053.1 nor Okla. R. Ct. Crim. App. 6.1, Okla. Stat. tit. 22, ch. 18, app., applied in that the city did not properly reserve a question of law in the district court and no statute was found unconstitutional. Finally, case law interpreting Okla. Stat. tit. 11, §§ 27-132, 28-128 had limited the right to appeal to a criminal defendant and not the state or municipality. Therefore, because the city did not meet the requirements of Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 1053 for an appeal by a municipality, the city's appeal was not properly before the court.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the city's appeal.

Marquez-Burrola v. State


Case Number: D-2003-1140


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2007 OK CR 14; 157 P.3d 749; 2007 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 14



April 17, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF GRADY COUNTY. THE HONORABLE RICHARD G. VAN DYCK, DISTRICT JUDGE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the District Court of Grady County (Oklahoma) convicting him of first-degree murder under Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.7(A) (2001) and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for killing his wife. On appeal, the court held that the record supported the trial court's conclusion that defendant was competent to stand trial because the information available at the time of trial from several experts, including one chosen by defendant, gave neither defense counsel nor the trial court any material reason to believe that defendant's competency was in doubt. The court held that the evidence was sufficient to support defendant's conviction because his history of jealousy, his conduct in the days preceding the homicide, his scheme to get his wife to accompany him alone after she had moved out, the presence of a large knife in his car, defendant's own statements, and the nature of the wounds inflicted all supported the jury's conclusion that defendant deliberately meant to kill his wife. Defense counsel was ineffective for failing to adequately present mitigation evidence during defendant's sentencing. Modification of defendant's death sentence was appropriate, as evidence supporting the continuing threat to society aggravating factor was weak, defendant had no known criminal record, and evidence of his mental illness was considerable.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was affirmed. His sentence was modified to life imprisonment without the possibility of parole.

Glossip v. State


Case Number: D-2005-310


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2007 OK CR 12; 157 P.3d 143; 2007 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 13



April 13, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Glossip v. Oklahoma, 552 U.S. 1167, 128 S. Ct. 1124, 169 L. Ed. 2d 952, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 1116 (2008)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion granted by Glossip v. Workman, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 52166 (W.D. Okla., May 26, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OKLAHOMA COUNTY. BEFORE THE HONORABLE TWYLA MASON GRAY, DISTRICT JUDGE.
Glossip v. State, 2001 OK CR 21, 29 P.3d 597, 2001 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 22 (Okla. Crim. App., 2001)



DISPOSITION: RICHARD EUGENE GLOSSIP, Appellant, was tried by jury for the crimes of Murder in the First Degree in Case No. CF-97-244 in the District Court of Oklahoma County before the Honorable Twyla Mason Gray, District Judge. Glossip was sentenced to death, and he perfected this appeal. Judgment and Sentence is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found appellant guilty of first-degree murder in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.7(A) (Supp. 1996), found the existence of the murder for remuneration aggravating circumstance pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.12(3) (Supp. 1996), and set the punishment at death. The District Court of Oklahoma County, Oklahoma, formally sentenced him in accordance with the jury verdict. This appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a motel manager, was convicted of hiring a maintenance worker, the perpetrator, to kill the owner. The perpetrator's corroborating testimony was sufficient direct evidence to support appellant's conviction on a theory of aiding and abetting, Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 172 (2001): the perpetrator was completely dependent on appellant for work, food, and housing; it was appellant's idea to kill the owner; and he offered the perpetrator a large sum of money to commit the crime. The State also pointed out other aspects of appellant's involvement, other than the money, which pointed to his guilt: motive, concealment of the crime, intended flight, and his control over the perpetrator. Additionally, there was sufficient evidence to support the sole aggravating circumstance of murder for remuneration. Appellant claimed that the murder was only a method to steal the money from the owner's car. The flaw in his argument was that no murder was necessary for him and the perpetrator to steal the money from the car. The appellate court's mandatory sentence review revealed that the death penalty was not imposed because of any arbitrary factor, passion, or prejudice.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court and sentence were affirmed.

Lookingbill v. State


Case Number: F-2005-1235


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2007 OK CR 7; 157 P.3d 130; 2007 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 8



March 20, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the District Court of Greer County (Oklahoma) convicting him of unlawful possession of paraphernalia and transportation of beer in an open container, and deferring for five years imposition of judgment and sentence for felony possession of controlled dangerous substances.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted after his vehicle was stopped at a checkpoint and the officer observed an open container of beer in the vehicle and saw defendant pull a marijuana pipe from his pocket. On appeal, the court held that defendant's motion to suppress was properly denied and therefore his convictions were affirmed. The officers testified that there was a significant problem of unlicensed drivers in the county and that was their stated purpose for planning the driver's license checkpoint. The State offered proof showing that the officers chose the site because it was the route most traveled in the county. The checkpoint was in a fixed location, in broad daylight, and in an open area where the officers could be easily seen. The officers planned to stop every passing car and, if everything was in order, detain motorists for less than a minute.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed. The order deferring imposition of judgment and sentence for felony possession of controlled dangerous substances was affirmed, provided that the district court was directed to correct the journal entry of its deferred sentencing order to reflect the merger of counts one and two.

Brumfield v. State


Case Number: F-2005-952


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2007 OK CR 10; 155 P.3d 826; 2007 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 7



March 20, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury for the District Court of Pushmataha County (Oklahoma) found defendant guilty of aggravated manufacture of a controlled dangerous substance (methamphetamine) under Okla. Stat. tit. 63, § 2-401(G)(3)(h) (2004), and unlawful possession of a controlled dangerous substance (methamphetamine) under Okla. Stat. tit. 63, § 2-402(B)(1) (2004). His 20 year sentence and two year sentence, respectively, were to be served concurrently. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Four officers arrived at defendant's home to execute a warrant. The first officer testified that he knocked on the door, identified the group as the police, and announced that they had a warrant to search the property. After waiting approximately 20 seconds with no response, a second officer busted open the front door with a sledge hammer, and three officers entered defendant's home. Defendant alleged that the search of his home violated the Fourth Amendment's knock-and-announce requirement. However, even if the execution of the warrant at defendant's home violated the Fourth Amendment's knock-and-announce requirement, the evidence obtained did not need to be suppressed. In addition, Okla. Stat. tit. 63, § 2-401(G)(3)(h) (2004) was not unconstitutionally vague, and it was appropriately applied to the entire contents of the liquid mixture containing methamphetamine found in defendant's home. Finally, defendant's convictions for both aggravated manufacture and unlawful possession, based entirely on the methamphetamine contained within the liquid in a single glass jar, violated Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 11 (2001). Thus, his conviction for possession of methamphetamine was improper.


OUTCOME: The court of criminal appeals affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence for aggravated manufacture of a controlled dangerous substance (methamphetamine). However, the court of criminal appeals reversed and dismissed his conviction for unlawful possession of a controlled dangerous substance (methamphetamine).

Stouffer v. State

US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Stouffer v. Oklahoma, 549 U.S. 1352, 127 S. Ct. 2060, 167 L. Ed. 2d 787, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 4149 (2007)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Dismissed by Stouffer v. Sirmons, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 75462 (E.D. Okla., Sept. 29, 2008)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Stouffer v. Workman, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 119524 (W.D. Okla., Oct. 17, 2011)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by Stouffer v. Workman, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 123806 (E.D. Okla., Aug. 29, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OKLAHOMA COUNTY. BEFORE THE HONORABLE JERRY R. BASS, DISTRICT JUDGE.
Stouffer v. Reynolds, 214 F.3d 1231, 2000 U.S. App. LEXIS 12461 (10th Cir. Okla., 2000)



DISPOSITION: BIGLER JOBE STOUFFER, Appellant, was tried by jury for the crimes of Murder in the First Degree and Shooting with Intent to Kill in Case No. CF-85-509 in the District Court of Oklahoma County before the Honorable Jerry Bass, District Judge. Appellant was sentenced to death, and life respectively and he perfected this appeal. Judgment and Sentence is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a new trial, a jury in the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), found appellant guilty of first-degree murder in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.7(A), and shooting with the intent to kill in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 642 (1981). Appellant was sentenced to life imprisonment for the crime of shooting with the intent to kill and to death for the crime of first-degree murder. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Although appellant raised 18 issues on appeal, the court found no basis to overturn either his convictions or sentences. As to his convictions, there was no reason to credit appellant's argument regarding the improper admission of hearsay evidence against him or his claim that the admission of certain expert testimony violated his rights to a fair trial and sentencing. And, while evidence that appellant had a physical fight with his girlfriend's former boyfriend, admitted under Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 2404(B) (2001), was, in fact, inadmissible character evidence, Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 2403 (2005), the improper introduction of the evidence was harmless. At sentencing, the evidence was sufficient to support the jury's finding of the "great risk of death to more than one person" and the "murder committed for the purpose of avoiding or prevent a lawful arrest or prosecution" aggravating circumstances because appellant shot his two victims with the same weapon, at the same place, and at essentially the same time, and the jury could have believed, beyond a reasonable doubt, that appellant killed his second victim so that there would be no witnesses to the shooting of the first victim.


OUTCOME: Appellant's convictions and sentences were affirmed.

 

261 of 423 DOCUMENTS


Warnick v. Booher


Case Number: CQ-2006-566


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2006 OK CR 41; 144 P.3d 897; 2006 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 43



September 29, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Warnick v. Booher, 425 F.3d 842, 2005 U.S. App. LEXIS 20303 (10th Cir. Okla., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The case was before the court on a certified question from the United States District Court for the Northern District of Oklahoma regarding petitioner prisoner's application for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner was serving a six-year sentence for the revocation of probation. He was then sentenced to two concurrent twenty-year sentences, which were ordered to run consecutive to the six-year sentence. The Oklahoma Department of Corrections audited the prisoner's case and made a correction to his rebill date after the six-year sentence had expired. The prisoner filed a petition for habeas corpus relief citing a violation of double jeopardy principals. A certified question was then submitted to the court. In answering the question, the court determined that the prisoner's rights under U.S. Const. amend. V and Okla. Const. art. II, § 21 had not been violated. The Oklahoma Legislature granted the Oklahoma Department of Corrections (DOC) the authority to carry out terms of imprisonment, and the only expectation a prisoner had was he was not required to serve more time than the sentence imposed. Due to the ongoing accumulation and/or reduction of credits throughout incarceration, there was no expectation of finality or that the sentence would have been discharged earlier. Any errors in DOC recordkeeping were not grounds for early release.


OUTCOME: The certified question was answered; the prisoner had no expectation of finality in his sentence such that a correction could not have been made.

Warner v. State

COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA

September 27, 2006, Decided

Transferred by, in part Lockett v. Evans, 2014 OK 28, 2014 Okla. LEXIS 35 (Okla., 2014)

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma) of first degree murder (Child Abuse) under Okla. Stat. tit. 21, §§ 701.7 and 843 (1991), and first degree rape under Okla. Stat. tit. 21, §§ 1111, 1114 (1991). The jury found two aggravating circumstances and defendant was sentenced to death on the murder count and 75 years imprisonment on the rape count.


OVERVIEW: The 11-month-old victim and her mother lived with defendant. After defendant was left alone with the children, the victim was found not breathing. The opinion testimony of an emergency room physician and physician's assistant specializing in child abuse assisted the jury in understanding the cause of the injuries found on the victim and her sibling. Stat. tit. 21, § 1111(A), given the expert testimony regarding the victim's injuries although there was no semen found. The court also found that defendant's statements to a detective were properly admitted pursuant to the Fourth, Fifth, and Sixth Amendments in that the police scrupulously honored defendant's invocation of his right to remain silent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court.

Coddington v. State


Case Number: D-2003-887


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2006 OK CR 34; 142 P.3d 437; 2006 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 35



August 16, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Oklahoma v. Coddington, 549 U.S. 1361, 127 S. Ct. 2032, 167 L. Ed. 2d 804, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 4283 (2007)
Subsequent appeal at, Motion granted by, Motion denied by Coddington v. State, 2011 OK CR 17, 2011 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 18 (Okla. Crim. App., 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OKLAHOMA COUNTY. THE HONORABLE JERRY BASS, DISTRICT JUDGE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma) convicting him of first-degree murder in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.7(A) (1996) and robbery with a dangerous weapon in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 801 (1991) and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for beating the victim to death with a claw hammer after he refused to lend defendant money to purchase more cocaine. Defendant robbed the victim of $ 525 and subsequently robbed five convenience stores to get money for cocaine. On appeal, the court held that the admission of defendant's confession and the physical evidence derived therefrom did not violate his constitutional rights because he failed to show that his Miranda rights waiver was not knowingly and voluntarily made. Defendant was read his Miranda rights, and he waived them when he was arrested for the robberies, and he volunteered statements about the homicide and initiated that discussion. During a videotape of the interrogation, defendant was not threatened, coerced, or promised anything, and the fact that defendant was fatigued and hungry from drug usage did not render his waiver involuntary. However, defendant's Fourteenth Amendment rights were violated when the trial court excluded the videotaped examination of defendant's mother from evidence during sentencing, and therefore, his death sentence was reversed. The jury was also improperly instructed to disregard relevant mitigating evidence.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed, but his death sentence was reversed, and the case was remanded for resentencing.

 

264 of 423 DOCUMENTS


Maxwell v. State


Case Number: C-2005-935


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2006 OK CR 33; 141 P.3d 564; 2006 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 34



August 8, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant entered an Alford plea of guilty to maintaining a house of prostitution within 1,000 feet of a church in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 1031(D) and was sentenced to imprisonment for five years, all suspended and without supervision. The District Court of Garfield County, Oklahoma, denied defendant's motion to withdraw his plea. Defendant filed a petition for certiorari.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant challenged the constitutionality of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 1031(D) (Supp. 2002) arguing that the statute's "special protection" of churches violated the Establishment Clause of the U.S. Constitution. The appellate court disagreed, holding that § 1031(D) did not violate the Establishment Clause as it had a secular legislative purpose, namely, to deter prostitution-related activities in areas where the public could be reasonably be expected to gather. The intent of § 1031(D) was to protect potentially vulnerable populations from exposure to prostitution-related activities was further evidenced by the fact that it applied equally to such activities that occurred within 1,000 feet of a school. The principal or primary effect of § 1031(D) was not the advancement of religion, but rather the deterring of prostitution, particularly in locations where vulnerable populations could reasonably be expected to gather. This provision did not advance religion generally or any specific faith in particular. Nor did it endorse religious belief or favor any particular faith community, and it did not foster excessive government "entanglement" with religion.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

265 of 423 DOCUMENTS


Dodson v. State


Case Number: F-2005-179


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2006 OK CR 32; 150 P.3d 1054; 2006 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 33



July 26, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF TULSA COUNTY. THE HONORABLE TOM C. GILLERT, DISTRICT JUDGE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District Court of Tulsa County, Oklahoma, convicted defendant of trafficking illegal drugs (methamphetamine), in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 63, § 415B (Supp. 2004), failure to obtain drug tax stamp, in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 68, § 450 (2001), and unlawful possession of marijuana (misdemeanor), in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 63, § 2-402(B)(2) (Supp. 2004). Defendant was sentenced accordingly. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that Okla. Const. art. II, § 30 prohibited the issuance of a warrant based upon future contingent events, making the house warrant illegal, and the fruits of its execution inadmissible at trial. The appellate court found that nothing in the provision required a showing that the evidence had to be in the place to be searched at the time the warrant issued. Nothing prohibited the issuance of an anticipatory search warrant, and it did not run afoul of Oklahoma's constitution. Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 1222 required that the object of the search be currently in the possession of the person or at the location to be searched. The anticipatory search warrant issued was invalid and impermissible on statutory grounds and did not set forth probable cause for the warrant to issue. The appellate court would not apply the good-faith exception to save the admission of evidence obtained pursuant to a search warrant which was not supported by probable cause. Because the evidence of drug trafficking, possession of marijuana, and failure to affix tax stamp was obtained pursuant to a statutorily invalid warrant, the trial court should have granted the motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded to the district court with instructions to dismiss.

 

266 of 423 DOCUMENTS


Malicoat v. State


Case Number: D-1998-151


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2006 OK CR 25; 137 P.3d 1234; 2006 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 25



June 19, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Malicoat v. State, 2000 OK CR 1, 992 P.2d 383, 2000 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 2 (Okla. Crim. App., 2000).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District Court of Grady County, Oklahoma, convicted defendant of first degree murder in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.7(C) (1991). Defendant was sentenced to death, and his applications for post-conviction relief and habeas corpus relief were denied. Defendant filed an objection to the State's setting of an execution date.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that Oklahoma's lethal injection protocol violated the Eighth Amendment prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment. The appellate court determined that defendant failed to show that the protocol was facially unconstitutional. Defendant did not suggest that the protocol was anything but humane and effective. Oklahoma and at least thirty-three other capital punishment states mandated lethal injection as the primary means of execution, suggesting that it comported with contemporary standards of decency. The appellate court in the past held that lethal injection per se was not unconstitutional. Oklahoma's execution protocol was constitutional on its face. The protocol provided that a physician was present, monitoring the execution, and thus each step of the process (including insertion of IV tubes and administration of the fluids) had to be performed to the physician's satisfaction. The appellate court would not speculate on whether mistakes may have been made in past executions.


OUTCOME: Defendant's request for stay of execution and setting execution date was denied.

Mitchell v. State


Case Number: D-2002-1427


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2006 OK CR 20; 136 P.3d 671; 2006 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 22



May 30, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Post-conviction relief denied at Mitchell v. State, 2010 OK CR 14, 2010 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 13 (Okla. Crim. App., 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OKLAHOMA COUNTY. THE HONORABLE SUSAN P. CASWELL, DISTRICT JUDGE.
Mitchell v. Gibson, 262 F.3d 1036, 2001 U.S. App. LEXIS 18297 (10th Cir. Okla., 2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma) that, on resentencing, sentenced him to death for murder based on the jury's finding of 2 aggravating circumstances, that the murder was especially heinous or cruel, and was committed for the purpose of avoiding lawful arrest or prosecution under Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.12(4) and (5) (1991); and sentenced him to imprisonment for armed robbery and car theft.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was also convicted in his original trial of sodomy and rape, but those convictions were vacated and the State chose not to reprosecute him for those crimes. On retrial, the jury again imposed the death penalty. On appeal, defendant raised numerous claims of error. The court held that the trial court erred in allowing the State to argue "rape" as the predicate crime for the avoid-arrest aggravator because the State's evidence was inadequate to support a rape conviction where there was no evidence of penetration. The court reversed the death penalty sentence based on its finding of cumulative error, which included the trial court's unwillingness to provide defendant any opportunity to voir dire jurors who expressed reservations about the death penalty or to rehabilitate jurors who expressed an unwillingness to consider life sentences. The court found that the trial court also erred in its admission of numerous photographs of the victim and a crime scene video, in excluding mitigating evidence, and in admitting victim impact testimony in addition to that of a family representative. The court found evidence of serious prosecutorial misconduct and significant trial court bias.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court finding of the avoid-arrest aggravator and reversed defendant's death sentence based on cumulative error. The court remanded the case with directions that it be reassigned to a new judge for resentencing because of the substantial evidence of trial court bias contained in the record. The court noted that the State was not precluded from re-pursuing the avoid-arrest aggravator in any future resentencing.

Hogan v. State


Case Number: D-2003-610


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2006 OK CR 19; 139 P.3d 907; 2006 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 20



May 15, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On rehearing at, Motion denied by Hogan v. State, 2006 OK CR 27, 2006 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 27 (Okla. Crim. App., 2006)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hogan v. Oklahoma, 549 U.S. 1139, 127 S. Ct. 994, 166 L. Ed. 2d 751, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 434 (2007)
Post-conviction proceeding at Hogan v. Sirmons, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 35216 (W.D. Okla., Apr. 30, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OKLAHOMA COUNTY. THE HONORABLE TAMMY BASS-JONES, DISTRICT JUDGE.
Hogan v. Gibson, 197 F.3d 1297, 1999 U.S. App. LEXIS 32107 (10th Cir. Okla., 1999)



DISPOSITION: KENNETH EUGENE HOGAN, Appellant, was tried by jury and convicted of First Degree Murder in the District Court of Oklahoma County, Case No. CRF-88-646. The Honorable Tammy Bass-Jones, District Judge presided and sentenced Hogan to death in accordance with the jury's verdict. Hogan perfected this appeal. Judgment and Sentence is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma) convicting him of first-degree murder and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for stabbing the victim to death. On appeal the court rejected defendant's argument that his first-degree murder conviction had to be modified to first-degree manslaughter on the ground that the State failed to prove beyond a reasonable doubt that he killed the victim with malice aforethought because a rational trier of fact could have found that defendant acted with malice aforethought beyond a reasonable doubt when he stabbed the victim 25 times. Defendant admitted he stabbed the victim multiple times and the evidence showed that defendant began his knifing of the victim near the kitchen, following her into the living room where he cut her throat, severing the carotid arteries on the left side of her neck. Defendant was not prejudiced by three references that were made to his previous trial because the jury was well aware that prior proceedings had taken place, as some of the testimony was presented by reading a transcript from defendant's prior trial. Any error was mitigated by the trial court's instructions. The admission of several photographs did not violate defendant's due process rights because they assisted the jury in understanding the crime scene.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and sentence were affirmed.

Browning v. State


Case Number: D-2003-363


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2006 OK CR 8; 134 P.3d 816; 2006 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 17



April 24, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at, Motion denied by Browning v. State, 2006 OK CR 37, 2006 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 38 (Okla. Crim. App., 2006)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Browning v. Okla., 2006 U.S. LEXIS 7668 (U.S., Oct. 10, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF TULSA COUNTY. THE HONORABLE REBECCA BRETT NIGHTINGALE, DISTRICT JUDGE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the District Court of Tulsa County (Oklahoma) convicting him of two counts of first-degree murder in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.7 (2001), shooting with intent to kill in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 652(A) (2001, first-degree arson in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 1401, and robbery with firearms in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 801 (2001), and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for killing his pregnant girlfriend's parents, attempting to kill his girlfriend, and setting the parents' home on fire. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not err by admitting into evidence photographs of duct tape and cigarette lighter fluid found at his residence because the facts alleged in the search warrant affidavit set forth probable cause to show that the items were at that location. After listing the crimes and defendant's participation, the affiant stated that, the day after the crimes, he saw items that appeared to be stolen from the victims' residence through defendant's window. The court rejected defendant's claim that the trial court abused its discretion by failing to either grant a mistrial or remove for cause a juror who was inappropriately contacted by a member of the victims' family because defendant failed to preserve the claim by excusing the juror with a peremptory challenge and making a record of which remaining juror he would have excused. The court dismissed defendant's convictions for arson and robbery because it treated defendant's convictions as ones for felony murder, and dismissed the underlying felonies.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions for first-degree arson and robbery with a firearm were dismissed. Defendant's other convictions were affirmed.

Rojem v. State


Case Number: D-2003-860


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2006 OK CR 7; 130 P.3d 287; 2006 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 7



February 24, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF WASHITA COUNTY. THE HONORABLE CHARLES L. GOODWIN, DISTRICT JUDGE.
Rojem v. State, 1988 OK CR 57, 753 P.2d 359, 1988 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 55 (Okla. Crim. App., 1988)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a resentencing proceeding, defendant appealed a judgment of the District Court of Washita County (Oklahoma) that again sentenced him to death after he was convicted of kidnapping, first-degree rape, and first-degree murder, based on the jury's finding of four aggravating circumstances: a prior violent felony; especially heinous, atrocious or cruel murder committed to avoid arrest or prosecution; and defendant was a continuing threat.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the jury's finding of the 4 aggravating circumstances and claimed the trial court erred by improperly denying his challenge for cause against prospective jurors; by disallowing two defense witnesses because his lawyers missed a discovery deadline; in its jury instructions on the issue of mitigation; and by precluding him from presenting evidence pertaining to "residual doubt" as a mitigating circumstance. The court held that defendant's due process rights were violated by the trial court's improper denial of his challenges for cause and its imposition of the overly harsh discovery sanction under Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 2002(E) (Supp. 2002) where the missed discovery deadline was not willful. The court found that the trial court refused to dismiss three jurors for cause, which resulted in prejudice when defendant was forced, over objection, to keep an unacceptable juror. One of the jurors was not qualified to serve under Okla. Stat. tit. 38, § 28(B)(4) (2001) because he had strong ties to law enforcement and worked as a jailer; and all three jurors had shown actual bias and were not qualified to serve under Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 659(2).


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's death sentence and remanded the case for a new sentencing proceeding consistent with the opinion.

 

271 of 423 DOCUMENTS


Lambert v. State


Case Number: PCD-2002-974


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2005 OK CR 26; 126 P.3d 646; 2005 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 20



December 7, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Oklahoma v. Pickens, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 7415 (U.S., Oct. 2, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Lambert v. State, 2003 OK CR 11, 71 P.3d 30, 2003 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 11 (Okla. Crim. App., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate sought review of the decision of the district court (Oklahoma), which determined that the inmate was not mentally retarded. He had been given two death sentences after being convicted of two counts of first-degree murder and was currently seeking post-conviction relief.


OVERVIEW: The inmate filed a supplemental brief after it was determined that he was not mentally retarded. The inmate proved all of the Murphy factors by a preponderance of the evidence. He had six IQ tests under 70. He had sub-average intellectual functioning and limitations in adaptive functioning in three uncontested skill areas. All those factors manifested before he was 18 years of ago. The jury determination that he was not mentally retarded was not supported by the record; no rational trier of fact, presented with that evidence, could have found that the inmate failed to meet his burden by a preponderance of the evidence. Although appellate courts give great deference to jury findings of fact, the court was compelled to conclude that, given the evidence presented, the determination that the inmate was not mentally retarded was not supported by the evidence. Additionally, the trial court's decision to deny individual voir dire led directly to a tainted jury panel.


OUTCOME: The court granted post-conviction relief and modified the inmate's two death sentences to two sentences of life imprisonment without the possibility of parole.

Harmon v. State


Case Number: F-2004-423


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2005 OK CR 19; 122 P.3d 861; 2005 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 15



October 18, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Harmon v. Jones, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 98468 (W.D. Okla., June 18, 2010)
Writ denied by, Request denied by Harmon v. Olsen, 2012 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 17 (Okla. Crim. App., Aug. 24, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was jointly tried, before the District Court of Garvin County, Oklahoma, and convicted of second degree burglary in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 1435 (2001) and of feloniously carrying a weapon in violation of Okla. Stat, tit. 21, § 1283 (2001). Defendant appealed his convictions and sentences.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and one of his children allegedly ransacked a house and stole property. Some of the property was given to defendant's daughter, which she hid and put into storage. On appeal, defendant correctly claimed that his trial counsel had a conflict of interest which interfered with his ability to receive a fair trial. Defense counsel represented defendant's daughter in her plea proceedings. Defendant pointed out that counsel had an actual conflict because he could not effectively cross-examine the daughter about her plea agreement and her role in the burglaries yet still maintain client confidentiality. The inherent conflict resulted in prejudice to defendant. The trial court also erred in failing to instruct the jury on the elements of feloniously carrying a weapon. Whether or not defendant agreed to the instructions, it was plain error to fail to instruct on the elements of a crime. The appellate court could not conclude, beyond a reasonable doubt, that this did not affect the jury's verdict.


OUTCOME: The appellate court concluded that the law and evidence required reversal. The matter was remanded for a new trial.

McCarty v. State


Case Number: PCD-2002-1493


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2005 OK CR 10; 114 P.3d 1089; 2005 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 9; 76 O.B.A.J. 1455



June 14, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by McCarty v. Oklahoma, 546 U.S. 1020, 126 S. Ct. 660, 163 L. Ed. 2d 534, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 8458 (2005)
Related proceeding at McCarty v. Gilchrist, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 13615 (W.D. Okla., Feb. 22, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: McCarty v. State, 1999 OK CR 24, 989 P.2d 990, 1999 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 56 (Okla. Crim. App., 1999)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed an application for post-conviction relief after being convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to death by the trial court (Oklahoma).


OVERVIEW: The inmate had been convicted and sentenced to death for first-degree murder. The case had a long procedural history, and currently before the court was one of his applications for post-conviction relief. The court concluded that it had merit because of testimony from an Oklahoma City Police chemist. The chemist withheld evidence; most likely lost or intentionally destroyed important and potentially exculpable (or incriminating) evidence; provided flawed laboratory analysis and documentation of her work; testified in a manner that exceeded acceptable limits of forensic science; and altered lab reports and handwritten notes in an effort to prevent detection of misconduct. As a result of the chemist's actions, the inmate did not receive a fair trial and resentencing proceeding. The record more than adequately supported the findings of the Oklahoma City Police Departmental Review Board pertaining to the chemist, including dishonesty in communications with her supervisor; mishandling evidence; failing to follow proper peer review procedure; and engaging in flawed and improperly documented casework analysis.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the conviction, vacated his death sentenced and remanded the case to the district court for a new trial.

 

274 of 423 DOCUMENTS


Whirlpool Corp. v. Henry


Case Number: CQ-2005-205


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2005 OK CR 7; 110 P.3d 83; 2005 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 6; 76 O.B.A.J. 811



March 28, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Williams Cos. v. Henry, 2004 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 27608 (N.D. Okla., Dec. 20, 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporations filed a civil suit in the Federal District Court for the Northern District of Oklahoma claiming that Okla. Stat. tit. 21, §§ 1289.7a and 1290.22(B) (Supp. 2004) were unconstitutional and sought injunctive relief. The federal district court certified a question of law to the Court of Criminal Appeals of Oklahoma.


OVERVIEW: The Governor of Oklahoma had signed into law an act which amended the Oklahoma Firearms Act and the Oklahoma Self-Defense Act, and proscribed certain persons from prohibiting the transporting and storing of firearms in locked vehicles on certain property. The corporations had established and implemented policies prohibiting the possession of firearms anywhere on company property, including inside vehicles parked in company parking lots. The company's sought injunctive relief to avoid being prosecuted under the new act. The court of criminal appeals initially noted that the plain statutory language of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, §§ 1289.7 and 1290.22(B) prohibited enumerated entities from establishing particular policies affecting firearms; that was, the plain language forbade an act. The court of criminal appeals held that the statutory language did not itself impose a punishment for performance of the forbidden acts, so the general misdemeanor statute controlled, and the offense was punishable as a misdemeanor, Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 21. The court of criminal appeals concluded that to find otherwise would have rendered Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 21 meaningless.


OUTCOME: The court of criminal appeals concluded that Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 1289.7a and Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 1290.22(B) (Supp. 2004) were both criminal statutes, the violation of which could have subjected a violator to criminal misdemeanor sanctions or punishment.

 

275 of 423 DOCUMENTS


Slaughter v. State


Case Number: PCD-2005-77


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2005 OK CR 6; 108 P.3d 1052; 2005 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 5; 76 O.B.A.J. 739



March 10, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Stay denied by Slaughter v. Okla., 161 L. Ed. 2d 297, 125 S. Ct. 1622, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 2408 (U.S., Mar. 15, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Slaughter v. State, 1997 OK CR 78, 950 P.2d 839, 1997 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 80 (Okla. Crim. App., 1997)



DISPOSITION: Application and motions denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner was convicted of two counts of first degree murder and sentenced to death. He filed two previous applications for state postconviction relief under Oklahoma's Post-Conviction Procedure Act, Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 1089 (2004), which were denied. This, his third application for such relief, raised the same issues as those raised in his second application.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner claimed that DNA evidence, developments in the field of comparative bullet analysis, and evidence of brain fingerprinting demonstrated that he was factually innocent of murder. He claimed that ineffective assistance of counsel on direct appeal resulted in his DNA claim being delayed. He raised constitutional challenges to § 1089 and Okla. Code Crim. P., Okla. Stat. tit. 22, ch. 18, § 9.7(G) (2004). Nothing had changed since the court disposed of the prisoner's evidentiary challenges in its second postconviction opinion. The prisoner failed to show any error by counsel that deprived him of effective assistance: the DNA evidence was not dispositive of the prisoner's factual innocence because the evidence of his guilt was strong even without the DNA evidence, and he was not considered a candidate for DNA testing until after the brain fingerprinting tests had been done. The court rejected the prisoner's challenges to Rule 9.7(G) and the Post-Conviction Procedure Act because there were no actual innocence claims in his postconviction filings that had been barred from review.


OUTCOME: The prisoner's third application for postconviction relief was denied.

Garrison v. State


Case Number: D-2001-1513


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2004 OK CR 35; 103 P.3d 590; 2004 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 37; 75 O.B.A.J. 3147



November 30, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied Garrison v. Workman, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 96912 (N.D. Okla., Aug. 29, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF TULSA COUNTY. THE HONORABLE JESSIE S. HARRIS, DISTRICT JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the District Court of Tulsa County (Oklahoma), which convicted him of first degree murder, a violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.7 (Supp. 1989). Defendant was sentenced to death upon finding the existence of two aggravating circumstances.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murdering a 13-year-old boy more than 10 years after the crime occurred. Defendant claimed that the delay deprived him of his due process rights. The court held: (1) while defendant claimed that some defense witnesses were dead or unavailable, the jury took into account the problems due to the passage of time, and so defendant's due process rights were not violated; (2) the evidence was sufficient to convict defendant in that defendant was seen with the boy going toward the lake where some of the body parts were found, and there were bite marks on defendant; and (3) a witness's description of defendant smelling like he had gutted a deer was not unduly prejudicial under Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 2403 (2001). The court reversed defendant's sentence, stating that defendant had a background that included severe abuse as a child and teenager and defendant's trial counsel did not present any of these potentially mitigating factors at trial, nor did his appellate counsel present evidence regarding his trial counsel's case in mitigation at the remanded evidentiary hearing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's sentence and remanded the matter for a new sentencing hearing.

Thacker v. State


Case Number: D-2003-21


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2004 OK CR 32; 100 P.3d 1052; 2004 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 34; 75 O.B.A.J. 3025



October 21, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Thacker v. Oklahoma, 544 U.S. 911, 125 S. Ct. 1611, 161 L. Ed. 2d 288, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 2338 (2005)
Post-conviction relief denied at, Motion denied by Thacker v. State, 2005 OK CR 18, 120 P.3d 1193, 2005 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 13 (Okla. Crim. App., 2005)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay granted by Thacker v. Sirmons, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 51517 (N.D. Okla., July 16, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF MAYES COUNTY. THE HONORABLE JAMES D. GOODPASTER, DISTRICT JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: District court judgments and sentences affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the District Court of Mayes County (Oklahoma), which convicted and sentenced him for first-degree murder (Count I), in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.7(A) (Supp. 1996), kidnapping (Count II), in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 741, and first-degree rape (Count III), in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 1114(A)(3) (1991).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to Counts I, II, and III and he was sentenced to death on the murder charge. He was sentenced to 10 years imprisonment on Count II and 50 years imprisonment on Count III. On appeal, the court affirmed, stating that defendant waived any claim of "irregularities" in the preliminary hearing proceedings by pleading guilty. Further, Oklahoma was not required to change its procedures and law so that aggravating circumstances should be included in an indictment or information and presented to a magistrate at the preliminary hearing. He also waived the right to a jury trial when he entered a plea of guilty and agreed to a non-jury trial on the issue of punishment. After reviewing the record and carefully weighing the aggravating circumstances and the mitigating evidence, along with the errors alleged in the appeal, the court found that the death sentence was factually substantiated and appropriate. The court could not say that the death sentence was imposed under the influence of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Dodd v. State


Case Number: D-2002-286


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2004 OK CR 31; 100 P.3d 1017; 2004 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 35; 75 O.B.A.J. 2999



October 21, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Dodd v. Oklahoma, 546 U.S. 835, 126 S. Ct. 62, 163 L. Ed. 2d 89, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 6226 (2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OKLAHOMA COUNTY THE HONORABLE TWYLA MASON GRAY, DISTRICT JUDGE.
Dodd v. State, 2000 OK CR 2, 993 P.2d 778, 2000 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 1 (Okla. Crim. App., 2000)



DISPOSITION: District court judgment and sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which convicted him of two counts of first-degree murder, in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.7(A) (1991), and which sentenced him to death. The two victims' throats had been slit.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that his constitutional rights were violated, that evidence was improperly admitted, and that insufficient evidence supported his conviction. In affirming, the court found that defendant had not shown that his right to be present had been violated. He also had not received ineffective assistance of counsel. The police had probable cause to place defendant under arrest. Admission of evidence of homicide literature in defendant's home was relevant and not unfairly prejudicial given the manner in which the victims were killed. Details of defendant's criminal history were directly relevant to his credibility as a witness. Sufficient evidence supported the conviction because the jury was presented with an interconnected web of physical evidence, eyewitness testimony, and defendant's inconsistent statements. The death sentence was not the product of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor because the jury found aggravating circumstances: the nature of the double murder itself and defendant's admission to a past crime of violence. The victim impact evidence did not distract the jury from rendering punishment out of a reasoned, moral response to the crimes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Lott v. State


Case Number: D-2002-88


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2004 OK CR 27; 98 P.3d 318; 2004 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 31; 75 O.B.A.J. 2385



September 9, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Lott v. Oklahoma, 544 U.S. 950, 125 S. Ct. 1699, 161 L. Ed. 2d 528, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 2828 (2005)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Lott v. Workman, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 35636 (W.D. Okla., Mar. 31, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OKLAHOMA COUNTY. THE HONORABLE VIRGIL C. BLACK, DISTRICT JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Convictions and sentences affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which convicted him of two counts of first-degree murder in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.7 (Supp. 1985) and sentenced him to death on each count.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of first-degree murder for the brutal killings of two elderly women. The jury recommended the sentence of death on each count and the trial court sentenced accordingly. The court affirmed, stating that it could not say that the jury was influenced by passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor contrary to Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.13(C) (2001), in finding that the aggravating circumstances outweighed the mitigating evidence. The trial court did not err in refusing to sever the two murder charges and try him separately for each offense because the evidence was sufficient to find that proof of each offense overlapped so as to evidence a common scheme or plan. Further, he failed to show any prejudice resulting from the joinder. The trial court did not err on instructing the jury on aiding and abetting because they were warranted by the evidence.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Duty v. State


Case Number: D-2002-1367


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2004 OK CR 20; 89 P.3d 1158; 2004 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 24; 75 O.B.A.J. 1289



April 23, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied, Request denied by Duty v. Sirmons, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 61090 (E.D. Okla., Aug. 17, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF PITTSBURG COUNTY. BEFORE THE HONORABLE STEVEN W. TAYLOR, DISTRICT JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to first-degree murder and the State's degree of particulars, and the District Court of Pittsburg County (Oklahoma) sentenced him to death. The appeal was a mandatory sentence review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant killed his prison cellmate, and, during the plea hearing, he was adamant about the fact that he would kill again. He pled guilty to first-degree murder and to the State's bill of particulars alleging four aggravating circumstances. The trial court concluded that defendant was competent and assessed the punishment at death. On capital mandatory sentence review, the court affirmed the judgment and sentence, stating that defendant was competent and had the capacity to understand the choice between life and death and to knowingly and intelligently waive any and all rights to appeal his sentence. The thorough competency examination by the physicians of the State hospital clearly indicated that he had the capacity to understand what he was doing and to knowingly and intelligently waive his appellate rights. Defendant was a long time prison resident who saw death as a better alternative to life without parole. The killing of his cellmate was a tool for his desire to die. Although it seemed that defendant was using the State to assist him in suicide, the death penalty was clearly indicated in the case, despite defendant's personal wishes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and sentence.

DeRosa v. State


Case Number: D-2001-1416


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2004 OK CR 19; 89 P.3d 1124; 2004 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 22; 75 O.B.A.J. 1267



April 22, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by DeRosa v. Oklahoma, 543 U.S. 1063, 125 S. Ct. 889, 160 L. Ed. 2d 793, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 39 (2005)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Request denied by, Judgment entered by Derosa v. Workman, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 102964 (E.D. Okla., Sept. 27, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF LAFLORE COUNTY. THE HONORABLE DOUG GABBARD, II, DISTRICT JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Convictions and sentences affirmed; case remanded for correction of judgment and sentence document.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the District Court of LeFlore County (Oklahoma), which convicted him of two counts of first-degree felony murder (robbery with a dangerous weapon), in violation of Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.7(B) (Supp. 2000). In accordance with the jury's recommendation, the trial court sentenced defendant to death on both counts.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and another man talked their way into the victims' rural home, stabbed them, and cut their throats, leaving them dead on the floor. The two men then stole some money and left in the victim's pickup truck. The court held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion by denying defendant's motion for change of venue after voir dire was completed. The trial court did not violate Witherspoon by striking one potential juror. Improper witness comments, victim impact testimony, and prosecutorial conduct were not unduly prejudicial. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in admitting photographs of the victims, which depicted the handiwork of defendant and his recruited cohort. The evidence was more than sufficient to establish the "avoid arrest" aggravator because defendant planned and accomplished the murders in order to avoid arrest and prosecution for robbing them. The court modified the uniform instruction on the "heinous, atrocious, or cruel" aggravating circumstance to more fully inform the jury regarding the findings that had to be made in order to properly apply the aggravator and to ensure that a jury determination was made regarding each of these findings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentences of death but remanded the case for correction of the judgment and sentence document to reflect the provision under which defendant was actually convicted.

283 of 423 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Lane v. Bass


No. PR-2003-946


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2004 OK CR 14; 87 P.3d 629; 2004 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 18; 75 O.B.A.J. 867



March 9, 2004, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Writ of Prohibition granted; remanded to district court with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with first-degree murder, among other charges. The District Court of Oklahoma County, Oklahoma, granted defendant's request for a separate jury trial on the issue of mental retardation prior to conducting defendant's trial for the murder charge. The State filed an application for writ of prohibition, alleging the trial judge was unauthorized to grant defendant's request.


OVERVIEW: On review, the appellate court found that the State was entitled to the relief requested as no prior case law required or authorized the trial court to empanel a separate jury trial on the issue of mental retardation before defendant was brought to trial on his murder charge. The appellate court also clarified how the issue of mental retardation could be resolved prior to trial by establishing a procedure for pretrial determination of mental retardation and for appeal. If the trial court denied the motion to quash bill of particulars on grounds of mental retardation, and if that decision was appealed and was affirmed on appeal, defendant's intellectual functioning could still be considered as a mitigating factor in the sentencing stage of the capital murder trial.


OUTCOME: The State's application for writ of prohibition was granted, and the matter was remanded to the trial court with instructions to vacate its order granting defendant's request for a separate jury trial on the issue of mental retardation and to proceed with defendant's trial.

 

284 of 423 DOCUMENTS


Littlejohn v. State


Case Number: D-2000-1609


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2004 OK CR 6; 85 P.3d 287; 2004 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 10; 75 O.B.A.J. 608



February 12, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Littlejohn v. Oklahoma, 543 U.S. 947, 125 S. Ct. 358, 160 L. Ed. 2d 261, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 7022 (2004)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Littlejohn v. Workman, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 52249 (W.D. Okla., May 27, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Littlejohn v. State, 1998 OK CR 75, 989 P.2d 901, 1998 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 69 (Okla. Crim. App., 1998)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in an Oklahoma trial court of robbery with a firearm, after former conviction of two or more felonies, first degree malice aforethought murder, and conspiracy to commit robbery with a firearm, after former conviction of two or more felonies. Defendant was sentenced to death. That sentence was reversed. Defendant was resentenced to death. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by allowing the State to read witnesses' testimony from his first trial at his resentencing without making a showing of unavailability. The court of appeals disagreed. The entire examination of both witnesses was read to the jury. The testimony had an adequate indicia of reliability as both witnesses had been thoroughly cross-examined by defense counsel. In addition, the testimony was taken under oath at the first trial and was transcribed by a licensed court reporter, thereby providing an adequate record. The cross-examination at the first trial was conducted by the same highly competent defense attorney who represented defendant at his resentencing trial. Defense counsel exposed the bias of both witnesses and challenged the accuracy of their perceptions and memories. In addition, their testimony was not the only evidence offered to show that defendant was the triggerman. The clerk's testimony corroborated the witnesses' testimony that defendant shot the victim as the victim approached him with a broom. Therefore, any error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Harris v. State


Case Number: D-2001-1268


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2004 OK CR 1; 84 P.3d 731; 2004 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 2; 75 O.B.A.J. 186



January 7, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected February 5, 2004.
Appeal after remand at Harris v. State, 2007 OK CR 28, 164 P.3d 1103, 2007 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 28 (Okla. Crim. App., 2007)
Post-conviction relief denied at Harris v. State, 2007 OK CR 32, 167 P.3d 438, 2007 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 32 (Okla. Crim. App., 2007)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Harris v. Royal, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 59395 (W.D. Okla., Apr. 19, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OKLAHOMA COUNTY. THE HONORABLE VIRGIL C. BLACK, DISTRICT JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed, sentence vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged in the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma) with first-degree murder, shooting with intent to kill, and assault and battery with a dangerous weapon. The jury found defendant guilty and recommended a sentence of death on the first count, life in prison on the second count, and 10 years' imprisonment on the third count. The trial court sentenced defendant in accordance with the recommendation. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: One morning, defendant entered his wife's workplace, shot and killed her employer, and seriously injured his wife. On appeal from his convictions and sentences, defendant argued that the evidence was insufficient to convict him of first degree murder and shooting with intent to kill. The court affirmed defendant's convictions, stating that the jury's rejection of defendant's diminished-capacity/intoxication defense was reasonable. The court also rejected defendant's claim that the evidence was insufficient to support the single aggravating circumstance found by the jury under Okla. Stat. tit. 21, § 701.12(2) (2001). The evidence showed that defendant shot the victim twice at close range inside a transmission shop, while his wife and another person stood a short distance away. However, the court vacated defendant's death sentence due to the trial court's instruction to the jurors in response to their question about where defendant might be assigned as a prisoner if he received a life sentence. The court stated that any instruction attempted by the judicial branch, about future actions of the executive or legislative branch in these areas, was doomed to inaccuracy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions. The court affirmed defendant's sentences on the second and third counts, but vacated the death sentence imposed on the first count and remanded for resentencing.

 

286 of 423 DOCUMENTS


Pitts v. State


Case Number: F-2003-540


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2003 OK CR 21; 78 P.3d 551; 2003 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 20; 74 O.B.A.J. 2883



October 10, 2003, Decided



DISPOSITION: Appeal is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a notice of intent to appeal and designation of record (notice and designation) in the District Court of Bryan County, Oklahoma. A petition in error was filed by defendant's counsel, Oklahoma Indigent Defense System (OIDS). Defendant sought to appeal a revocation order. OIDS counsel moved to dismiss the pending appellate proceeding, as defendant's trial counsel had not filed the required notice and designation within 10 days.


OVERVIEW: In a regular direct appeal from a conviction, a defendant's time limitation for commencing an appeal began from the date of pronouncement of judgment and sentence. Appellate review of an order revoking a suspended sentence followed the same procedure as that for perfecting a regular misdemeanor or felony appeal. Provided, however, that in a revocation appeal, for the purpose of calculating the date that the appellate time period would commence, the date of the trial court's imposition of the order of revocation was substituted for the date of judgment and sentence. Consequently, in defendant's matter, his notice and designation were required to be filed within 10 days from the date the district court pronounced its decision to revoke. In defendant's case, it was not until Tuesday, May 20, 2003, that defendant filed with the trial court clerk his notice and designation. This filing occurred more than 10 calendar days after the Wednesday, May 7th, imposition of the order of revocation. Thus, unless something other than calendar days were counted in calculating the 10-day filing deadline, defendant's attempted appeal was not timely initiated.


OUTCOME: Defendant's matter was dismissed.

 

287 of 423 DOCUMENTS


Lambert v. State


Case Number: PCD-2002-974


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2003 OK CR 11; 71 P.3d 30; 2003 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 11; 74 O.B.A.J. 1753



May 29, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at Lambert v. State, 2005 OK CR 26, 126 P.3d 646, 2005 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 20 (Okla. Crim. App., 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Lambert v. State, 1999 OK CR 17, 984 P.2d 221, 1999 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 46 (Okla. Crim. App., 1999)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate was convicted in the District Court of Creek County (Oklahoma), as charged by appellee State, of first degree murder. He was sentenced to death. The inmate filed a successor application for post-conviction relief and requested an evidentiary hearing on the issues of mental retardation. The court remanded for an evidentiary hearing, then held the hearing in abeyance and ordered the State to respond to the application.


OVERVIEW: The inmate sought an evidentiary hearing on, among other things, mental retardation. The court concluded that the claim of mental retardation was to be resolved pursuant to the definition of the term as set forth in case law. Because the inmate raised sufficient evidence to create a question of fact on the issue of mental retardation, the court remanded the case to the trial court for a jury determination on the question. The court addressed the procedures for the post-conviction determination of mental retardation on remand: (1) the hearing was to be conducted after full discovery was afforded both parties under the Oklahoma Criminal Discovery Code, Okla. Stat. tit. 22, § 2001 et seq. (2001); (2) the trial court was to empanel a jury; (3) the inmate was to present evidence first; and (4) each party was entitled to have the inmate examined by an expert. The jury was to indicate its finding on a verdict form, which did not violate Okla. Const. art. VII, § 15 because such a jury finding was not a verdict. The jury's finding was to determine the validity of the inmate's sentence.


OUTCOME: The court granted the inmate's petition for post-conviction relief and remanded to the trial court for a jury determination on the issue of mental retardation.

McElmurry v. State


Case Number: D-2000-884


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2002 OK CR 40; 60 P.3d 4; 2002 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 47; 73 O.B.A.J. 21



December 2, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected February 26, 2003.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgments and sentences of death entered in the McIntosh County District Court, Oklahoma, on two counts of first degree murder with malice aforethought together with 100 years' imprisonment for robbery with a dangerous weapon, and, in a second case, 20 years' imprisonment for larceny of a motor vehicle. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who had been granted probation, carried out with his wife a plan to rob and kill two weak and elderly persons who had recently employed them. On appeal defendant asserted inter alia that the district court should have instructed on the defense of "voluntary intoxication" based on defendant's testimony at the preliminary hearing that before the murder he and his wife had injected some methamphetamine. The court sustained the district court's refusal to give the intoxication instruction, noting that after committing the murder, defendant was able to drive 400 miles in the victim's car and six days later was able later to clearly describe the events surrounding the murder. Defendant also contended that a complete and adequate review of his case could not be conducted because there were several instances where the transcript of voir dire did not indicate the names of the prospective jurors who were being addressed or who were responding to questions from the judge or counsel. The court reviewed for plain error, holding that defendant had waived any objections to the nature and extent of voir dire not made before the commencement of testimony.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the jury's determination that the aggravating circumstances outweighed the mitigating evidence and that the sentence of death was factually substantiated and appropriate and not imposed under the influence of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor, and, finding no error warranting reversal or modification, affirmed the judgments and sentences entered in the district court.

Modified and rehearing denied by Grant v. State, 2003 OK CR 2, 2003 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 2 (Okla. Crim. App., 2003)
Vacated by, Remanded by, Motion granted by Grant v. Oklahoma, 540 U.S. 801, 124 S. Ct. 162, 157 L. Ed. 2d 12, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 5440 (2003)

Torres v. State


Case Number: PCD-2002-1047


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2002 OK CR 35; 58 P.3d 214; 2002 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 40; 110 A.L.R.5th 641; 73 O.B.A.J. 3303



October 15, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Torres v. Oklahoma, 538 U.S. 928, 155 L. Ed. 2d 323, 123 S. Ct. 1580, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 2283 (2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Torres v. State, 1998 OK CR 40, 962 P.2d 3, 1998 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 40 (Okla. Crim. App., 1998)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellant's subsequent application for post-conviction relief was denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner was convicted of first degree murder and other charges and was sentenced to death. Before the court was petitioner's second application for post-conviction relief.


OVERVIEW: After petitioner's conviction was affirmed on appeal, and the court denied petitioner's first application for post-conviction relief, petitioner filed another application. The court denied the application. The court was not entitled to consider the merits of a subsequent application for such relief, unless the application contained sufficient specific facts establishing that the current issues had not and could not have been previously presented. Petitioner argued that state jurors should have been instructed that aggravating circumstances had to outweigh mitigating evidence beyond a reasonable doubt. The court held that petitioner misconstrued the state death penalty statutes. The substantive element of capital murder was the jury's finding of an aggravated circumstance necessary to support a capital sentence. This finding authorized jurors to consider imposing the death penalty, not the weighing of all circumstances, as contended by petitioner. The only thing that case law required was that the jurors make the factual finding of an aggravating circumstance beyond a reasonable doubt, and, once this was found, the substantive elements of the capital crime were satisfied.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's application.

Malone v. State


Case No. F-2000-1585


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF OKLAHOMA


2002 OK CR 34; 58 P.3d 208; 2002 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 39; 73 O.B.A.J. 2933



October 14, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 23, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: AN APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF TULSA COUNTY, OKLAHOMA. THE HONORABLE J. MICHAEL GASSETT.
 Malone v. State, 2002 OK CR 14, 2002 Okla. Crim. App. LEXIS 12 (2002)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury of the Tulsa County District Court, Oklahoma, convicted defendant of first degree burglary (count I), first degree rape (count II), and sexual battery (count III). Defendant was sentenced to 20 years' imprisonment and a $ 10,000 fine on count I, 50 years' imprisonment and a $ 10,000 fine on count II, and 20 years' imprisonment and a $ 10,000 fine on count III. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that he was denied a fair sentencing proceeding when the trial court precluded the admission of mitigating evidence relevant to the jury's exercise of discretion. The instant court rejected defendant's argument. It also ordered that its summary opinion of March 18, 2002, be stricken, removed from publication, and superceded by this opinion on rehearing in its entirety, including all matters pertinent to the issue defendant raised in his original appeal. The instant court concluded that defendant was not denied a fair sentencing hearing by the trial judge's decision to preclude defendant from presenting mitigating evidence to the jury at his non-capital sentencing proceeding. A criminal trial was not to be based upon so-called "character" evidence, and the same principle applied to sentencing proceedings.


OUTCOME: The judgments and sentences were affirmed.
